♠ respect for property ♣ present ♥ “The church, at least from the 10th century, imposed on thieves severe fasts, compulsory almsgiving, and exclusion from communion for one or two years.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_892_TrByzM2]
♠ Moralistic punishment by human agents ♣ present ♥ There were two types of punishment that religious agents or institutions could impose on the faithful: epitimia and excommunication. The epitimion was “a penalty imposed on a penitent by the priest following sacramental confession. The term was already in use by the 4th C. (Basil the Great, PG 32:721A). As a rule these penitential exercises, mentioned in Byz. canonical and ascetical literature, presupposed repentance and consisted of prayer, fasting, Scripture reading, prostrations, almsgiving, and, on occasion, temporary exclusion from the Eucharist. They were distinguished by their largely positive character and relative mildness from such formal punishments (‘’timoriai’’) as excommunication, suspension, or deposition, which were inflicted by the church for more serious transgressions such as heresy or apostasy. Since sin was understood as a disease rather than a legally punishable crime, ‘’epitimia’’ in Byz. penitential practice and theology were viewed as corrective remedies, that is, as a form of spiritual healing. At any rate, they were never reduced to a payment or a fine due to God.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_893_TrByzM2] As for excommunication, it “entailed the exclusion of the transgressor from the community or fellowship of the church and its sacraments, esp. the Eucharist. Offending members included emperors, for example, Leo VI and Michael VIII. The separation from the church’s sacramental life was either absolute or partial, that is, it could be either temporary or for the lifetime of the individual.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_894_TrByzM2][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Type of punishment this typically entailed (can be more than one):
♠ physical punishment ♣ present ♥ Fasting was commonly included in the epitimia that were “imposed on a penitent by the priest following sacramental confession.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_895_TrByzM2]
♠ exile ♣ inferred absent ♥ Based on the following quote, it seems that exile was a form of punishment that could be inflicted by the state, and not by religious agents—indeed, it was commonly inflicted ‘’upon’’ religious agents. “The Book of the Eparch several times mentions ‘’exoria’’ as a penalty for economic crimes; normally, however, exile was reserved for political criminals and suspects, esp. church leaders (John Chrysostom, Pope Martin I, Theodore of Stoudios, Photios, etc.).” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_896_TrByzM2] Excommunication “entailed the exclusion of the transgressor from the community or fellowship of the church and its sacraments, esp. the Eucharist.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_897_TrByzM2] but the relevant entry of the ‘’Oxford Dictionary of Byzantium’’ does not say that this exclusion was physical.
♠ loss/seizure of property or wealth ♣ inferred absent ♥ Epitimia, commonly “imposed on a penitent by the priest following sacramental confession [...] were never reduced to a payment or a fine due to God.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_898_TrByzM2] Similarly, the relevant entry of the ‘’Oxford Dictionary of Byzantium’’ [MAJIDBENAM_REF_899_TrByzM2] does not say that excommunication included the loss or seziure of property.
♠ loss of status ♣ present ♥ Excommunication “entailed the exclusion of the transgressor from the community or fellowship of the church and its sacraments, esp. the Eucharist.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_900_TrByzM2][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Extent of punishment (can be more than one):
♠ punishment of rulers ♣ present ♥ Excommunication “entailed the exclusion of the transgressor from the community or fellowship of the church and its sacraments, esp. the Eucharist. Offending members included emperors, for example, Leo VI and Michael VIII.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_901_TrByzM2]
♠ punishment of elites ♣ inferred present ♥ There were two types of punishment that religious agents or institutions could impose on the faithful: epitimia and excommunication. Because even emperors could be subjected to the latter, graver form of punishment, it seems reasonable to assume that elites could also be excommunicated or forced to undergo an epitimion. The epitimion was “a penalty imposed on a penitent by the priest following sacramental confession. The term was already in use by the 4th C. (Basil the Great, PG 32:721A). As a rule these penitential exercises, mentioned in Byz. canonical and ascetical literature, presupposed repentance and consisted of prayer, fasting, Scripture reading, prostrations, almsgiving, and, on occasion, temporary exclusion from the Eucharist. They were distinguished by their largely positive character and relative mildness from such formal punishments (‘’timoriai’’) as excommunication, suspension, or deposition, which were inflicted by the church for more serious transgressions such as heresy or apostasy. Since sin was understood as a disease rather than a legally punishable crime, ‘’epitimia’’ in Byz. penitential practice and theology were viewed as corrective remedies, that is, as a form of spiritual healing. At any rate, they were never reduced to a payment or a fine due to God.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_902_TrByzM2] As for excommunication, it “entailed the exclusion of the transgressor from the community or fellowship of the church and its sacraments, esp. the Eucharist. Offending members included emperors, for example, Leo VI and Michael VIII. The separation from the church’s sacramental life was either absolute or partial, that is, it could be either temporary or for the lifetime of the individual.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_903_TrByzM2]

Economic Well-Being[edit]

State Intervention in Economy
♠ The state regulates banking ♣ ♥ Bankers (argyropratai). "The importance of the money market to the Byzantine economy made state control here also essential. Thus the states supervised the rate of interest demanded and laid down compulsory regulations for this."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_919_TrByzM2] Guilds "set the rules about banking, and secular and canon law strictly regulated the banking, moneylending and money-changing businesses, so they were always relatively modest in scale, and credit was rarely extended to individuals."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_920_TrByzM2] Bankers and moneylenders (trapezites and argyroprates). By 10th CE guilds existed for bankers and moneylenders. Prohibitions existed on both moneylending and moneychanging "particularly for clergy, although monasteries did own moneychanging businesses."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_921_TrByzM2] The 'Book of the Eparch' under Leo VI (886-912 CE) "provided rules and regulations for trade and trade organizations in Constantinople.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_922_TrByzM2]
♠ Price controls ♣ present ♥ Bankers (argyropratai). "The importance of the money market to the Byzantine economy made state control here also essential. Thus the states supervised the rate of interest demanded and laid down compulsory regulations for this."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_923_TrByzM2] Present, especially in the capital. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_924_TrByzM2]
♠ Regulations against hoarding/stockpiling goods ♣ ♥ Michael VII (1071-1078 CE) and his administrator Nikephoritzes attempted to regulate the grain supply of Constantinople and decreased "the size of the modios, the main Byzantine measure of grain, by a pinakion (about a quarter of a modius)".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_925_TrByzM2]
♠ Regulations regarding usury ♣ ♥
♠ Debt-relief measures ♣ ♥
♠ Pensions ♣ ♥
♠ Standardization of weights/measures ♣ ♥ "The metrological system employed throughout most of the Byzantine period was a duodecimal one. The linchpin of this system was the Byzantine pound or litra, derived from the late Roman pound. The litra was divided into 12 ounces, the ounce into multiples of the scripulum, the smallest unit of the libral system." The litra was about 327.60 in grams. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_926_TrByzM2] "Chapter 15 of Novel 128 of Justinian states that the praetorian prefect and the eparch of the city were responsible for commodity weights and the comes sacrorum largitionum for coinage weights of gold, silver, or bronze." However, the role of this official may have declined. The eparch of Constantinople "was certainly responsible for the issuance of glass weights during the sixth and seventh centuries, and, by the ninth century, according to the Book of the Eparch, his control of all forms of weights and measures in the capital was absolute. In reality, as the inscriptions on surviving weights indicate, other officials not mentioned in imperial legislation also issued weights. In the western half of the empire these included various proconsuls, viri laudables, and viri clarissmi; in the East the titles anthypatos, comes, and ephoros have been recorded."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_927_TrByzM2]
♠ Guarantee stability of coin value ♣ ♥ Solidus gold coin introduced by Constantine I "remained the basis for the Byzantine precious metal coinage until the Latin conquest in 1204. ... its fractions were 12 silver hexagrams or milliaresia and 288 copper folleis. From the middle of the 11th century increasingly depreciated..."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_928_TrByzM2] Miliaresion: 7th-11th centuries silver coin at rate of 144 to 108 to the pound.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_929_TrByzM2] 1034-1041 CE: "Michael IV takes first steps in debasement of gold currency."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_930_TrByzM2] "The most remarkable aspect of the gold coinage was its stability. The soildus was originally struck under Constantine I at a weight of 72 coins to a pound of gold ... Through the subsequent 700 years there was no significant variation in the value of the nomisma (as it came more commonly to be called)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_931_TrByzM2] However currency was devalued when Nikephoros Phokas (963-969 CE) began a line of coinage 1/12th lighter than the solidus, which was used to pay salaries and debts, which "had the effect of devaluing the currency." "[MAJIDBENAM_REF_932_TrByzM2] Direct form of devaluation began in the eleventh century from Michael IV (1034-1041 CE) when the amount of gold in coins was decreased.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_933_TrByzM2]

Public Goods[edit]

♠ Famine relief ♣ present ♥ Xenodocheion were used to organise a response to famine.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_935_TrByzM2] Present in some cases, but depending on the policy and means of the government.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_936_TrByzM2]
♠ provision of famine relief ♣ inferred state ♥ Present in some cases, but depending on the policy and means of the government.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_937_TrByzM2]
♠ Food storage ♣ present ♥ In the 6th century CE "Justinian built an enormous storehouse at the mouth of the Hellespont, 90 feet by 280 feet; and 'ineffably' high. ... There were also at least four other granaries dating from earlier times, and other cities such as Alexandria had their massive storehouses as well."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_938_TrByzM2] Castella settlements on the lower Danube "had common granaries for corn". [MAJIDBENAM_REF_939_TrByzM2] Such as in Constantinople: "Two granaries near the Marmara, the Alexandrina and Theodosianum, stored some of the grain from Egypt, while some was held in three granaries to the north, near the Srategion and Prosphorion harbour."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_940_TrByzM2]
♠ provision of food storage ♣ ♥
♠ Alimentary supplementation ♣ present ♥ Xenodocheion (or ptochotropheia) were established by bishops and wealthy Christians to provide a place of rest and food to poor travellers and the homeless. They first appeared from the mid-fourth century CE.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_941_TrByzM2] "The tribute that Egypt paid was tribute in grain - about 240 metric tons per year. This vast amount of wheat was brought in to Constantinople for the bread dole - central to the extensive social welfare system in the city."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_942_TrByzM2]

♠ Drinking water ♣ present ♥ "Over 150 covered cisterns and reservoirs survive of the complex water programme, the most impressive of which is the Basilica Cistern (Yerebatansaray) (Crow and Bayliss 2005)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_943_TrByzM2]
♠ provision of drinking water ♣ ♥
♠ Irrigation works ♣ present ♥ Preiser-Kapeller says present.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_944_TrByzM2] Territory of Egypt and Syria not held in this period. However Harvey (2008): "Landowners had the resources to make improvements to their properties, in particular the construction of irrigation systems, and to specialize in cash crops like vines and olives."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_945_TrByzM2]Large landowners "had the resources to effect large-scale irrigation works and to spend large sums on viticulture and arboriculture, which did not bring returns for several years. These improvements added to the revenues from agriculture."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_946_TrByzM2]
♠ provision of irrigation works ♣ ♥
♠ Health infrastructure ♣ present ♥' "the protonotarius had to cover the expenses of the theme. ... These outgoings included the soldiers' pay, the salaries of officers and officials as well as expenditure for the upkeep of public welfare services, such as geriatric homes, orphanages and infirmaries."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_947_TrByzM2] Byzantine hospitals were usually called xenones. Began as almshouses in 4th CE then became dedicated to medical care. "By the reign of the emperor Justinian (527-565), the best physicians of the empire dedicated a substantial portion of their professional efforts to curing indigent patients in these xenones. During the centuries that followed, these hospitals expanded their services and by the eleventh and twelth centuries had become the major centers of health care in Constantinople and perhaps in other large Byzantine towns such as Thessalonike and Nicaea."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_948_TrByzM2] By 1100 CE, out-patient services. All classes sought and received treatment in Byzantine hospitals.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_949_TrByzM2]
♠ Control of infectious disease ♣ ♥
♠ Care of war invalids/veterans ♣ present ♥ Since East Roman Empire, extensive welfare system "supported by the imperial government, the Orthodox Church, the monastic movement, and private persons. Several monographs have described the network of almshouses (xenodocheia or diakoniai), old-age homes (gerokomeia), medical hospitals (nosokomeia or xenones), and orphanages (orphanotropheia) that East Roman society maintained to aid all kinds of needy people."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_950_TrByzM2]
♠ Care of orphans ♣ present ♥' "the protonotarius had to cover the expenses of the theme. ... These outgoings included the soldiers' pay, the salaries of officers and officials as well as expenditure for the upkeep of public welfare services, such as geriatric homes, orphanages and infirmaries."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_951_TrByzM2] Present since the East Roman Empire, although "most state activity on behalf of orphans focused on reforming guardianship laws rather than on building large institutions to house and educate homeless children."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_952_TrByzM2] There was a director of the Orphanotropheion (a state-run orphanage) in Constantinople (the Orphanotrophoi).[MAJIDBENAM_REF_953_TrByzM2]
♠ Care of elderly ♣ present ♥ "the protonotarius had to cover the expenses of the theme. ... ♠ Currency ♣ ♥ Monetization had increased significantly from Theophilus (813-842 CE) "who increased the gold coins in circulation by nearly doubling the army's pay, and minted copper coins at about six times the previous rate. Although copper coins were a small part of the total money supply, they were important for everyday transactions, which could now be conducted with money instead of by barter."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_984_TrByzM2] Early Middle Ages: peasants "were primarily engaged in subsistence farming and were involved in commerce only on a very limited scale to obtain the cash required for their tax-payments."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_985_TrByzM2][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Debt and Indemnities

Taxation[edit]

♠ Degree of taxation ♣ ♥Elatikon: "supplementary tax additional to the demosion and exacted at a flat rate".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_988_TrByzM2][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Epibole: "the rate of epibole was the equation between the area of a fiscal unit and the basic land-tax imposed on it (excluding the supplementary taxes)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_989_TrByzM2][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Synetheia: "supplementary tax. imposed at a flat rate in addition to the basic land-tax".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_990_TrByzM2][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Telos: "tax-payment".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_991_TrByzM2][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Taxes are imposed on:
♠ Land ♣ ♥ "The Byzantine surveyor, the anagrapheus, the official responsible for the precise valuation of land for purposes of taxation"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_992_TrByzM2] Hexafollon: "flat-rate surcharge imposed with the land-tax".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_993_TrByzM2] Ennomion: "a tax on the pasture of animals".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_994_TrByzM2] "The taxes on the abandoned property were claimed from the remaining villagers through the collective responsibility of the members of the fiscal unit for its tax-burden. Consequently, more land was accumulated by the wealthier villages, who had the means to cultivate the land and pay the taxes."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_995_TrByzM2]
♠ Property ♣ ♥ John the Orphanotrophos "essentially administered the state" for Emperor Michael I (1034-1041 CE) and imposed "ruthless taxation policies... John sought to tax the substantial wealth of the dynatoi, and for this he earned the support of the civil aristocracy, but his measures naturally were felt more keenly by the poor than by the wealthy, and he did nothing effective to restrict the disappearance of peasant landholding in the provinces."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_996_TrByzM2] Kapnikon: "a tax on household".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_997_TrByzM2]
♠ People ♣ ♥
♠ Trade ♣ ♥ Customs building. "The route by which oriental goods came is well known. All ships coming from Syria and Egypt had to go to Attaleia, the great harbour in south-west Asia Minor. Only then were they allowed to continue their journey to Constantinople. At Atteleia the customs officials came on board and entered against the list of goods the duty payable to the customs. The rate of duty was very high."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_998_TrByzM2] Kommerkion: "a tax of a tenth on commercial transaction"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_999_TrByzM2]

♠ executive or ruling elite ♣ ♥
♠ local elites ♣ ♥ "the great majority of Byzantines lived in villages. Since these, like the cities, had become independent tax districts with their own assessments, the government tried to protect the poorer villagers from being bought out or forced to pay more that their share by their richer neighbors."[1,001] In about 1025 CE the total of people directly employed by Byzantine state - officials, officers, soldiers, oarsmen - together with their families likely "exceeded a million souls."[1,002] A consequence of this may have been that those individuals who had no links to the state "had no superior officer or official to protect them, were easily exploited by bureaucrats and especially by tax collectors."[1,003]
♠ state officials ♣ ♥ "The taxes collected by the tax-collectors (dioketai) in the various tax zones were paid into the chartularius' office, entered and checked and then handed over to the protonotarius' office."[1,004] "Some officials came from banking circles (argyropratai). They were entrusted with book-keeping and accounts, and had to spend many years as money-changers or transacting loans before they ventured to jump into state service. They mostly got posts in the taxation department, first in provincial administration, and then, if they were successful, they would be recalled to the central offices in Constantinople."[1,005][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Type of taxes paid:

Wages and Costs[edit]

♠ Wages ♣ ♥
♠ staple cost of principal source of carbohydrates ♣ ♥
♠ Top fortune ♣ ♥
♠ Other wealth distribution ♣ ♥ ♠ Dedicated police force ♣ present ♥ In Constantinople the chief of police was the city prefect - eparch - (also the chief justice), who was directly appointed by the emperor, who worked out of an office building called the Praetorium. His staff included a range of powerful officials whose responsibilities ranged from fortification to street cleaning, from maintenance for baths and the hippodrome to water management. They sponsored markets and churches, monitored the scales, measurements and weights, issued regulations for buildings and guilds, maintained public order and provided a response to fires. The Praetorium was also the main police station which was close to courts and a prison. Legatarioi were officials who had a role in policing. They wee appointed by the exarch (with the emperor’s approval). Their duties included interdiction of smuggled goods and registering foreigners who entered Constantinople and ensured they did not stay more than 3 months. Armed officers called 'collegiati' were present on the streets armed with clubs and maces.[1,007] [1,008] [1,009]
♠ Police levels ♣ 5 ♥ The Emperor (1) appointed an Exarch - city prefect - of Constantinople (2) who ran a specialist administrative building (Praetorium) which contained high officials who worked on many aspects of city life. There were likely offices within the Praetorium dedicated to security and/or law and order (3). High officials appointed by the exarch included the symponoi and legatarioi. Policing also involves white collar crimes like fraud so market inspection which may have been run out of a different office and had a separate chain of authority could be considered police work. There were probably sub-stations around and outside the city (4) as the exarch's authority extended beyond the city. At the lowest level were the 'collegiati' patrol men (5) who were armed with clubs and maces. The chain-of-command for market inspection is known to have five levels: Baileus - eparchos - symponos - legatarios - boullotes (the last in line policed instruments used for measuring and weighing and checked quantities that had the official seal).[1,010] [1,011] [1,012]
♠ Proscriptions of elites and or property ♣ ♥ Use of the state to repress or purge rival elite groups.
♠ Corporal punishment by the state ♣ ♥
♠ Military control over independent or semi-independent vassal states ♣ present ♥ "A game of competing nominal suzerainty over Dalmatian coastal cities ensued between Venice and Croatia, dying down only in 1019, when Byzantium reclaimed all imperial Dalmatia following Bulgaria's final defeat in the wars, and Croatia became a Byzantine vassal.”[1,013] Basil II: "Armenia was in part annexed to the Empire and in part reduced to vassal dependence; Bulgaria was transformed into a Byzantine province”.[1,014]
